<tr>
<th scope="row"><a href="#Downgrade_attacks">Protocol downgrade</a>
</th>
<td style="background:#FFC7C7;color:black;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center;" class="table-no">4.1%<br />Downgrade defence not supported
</td>
<td data-sort-value="" style="background: var(--background-color-interactive, #ececec); color: var(--color-base, inherit); vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;" class="table-na"><span aria-hidden="true"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1152813436" /><span class="sr-only">N/a</span>
</td>
<td style="background: #BFD; color:black; vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;" class="yes2 table-yes2">80.2%<br />Downgrade defence supported
</td>
<td style="background: var(--background-color-interactive, #EEE); color: var(--color-base, black); vertical-align: middle; text-align: center;" class="table-Unknown">15.7%<br />unknown
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Forward_secrecy">Forward secrecy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Forward secrecy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Forward_secrecy" title="Forward secrecy">Forward secrecy</a></div>
<p>Forward secrecy is a property of cryptographic systems which ensures that a session key derived from a set of public and private keys will not be compromised if one of the private keys is compromised in the future.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>167<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Without forward secrecy, if the server's private key is compromised, not only will all future TLS-encrypted sessions using that server certificate be compromised, but also any past sessions that used it as well (provided that these past sessions were intercepted and stored at the time of transmission).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>168<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An implementation of TLS can provide forward secrecy by requiring the use of ephemeral <a href="/wiki/Diffie%E2%80%93Hellman_key_exchange" title="DiffieHellman key exchange">DiffieHellman key exchange</a> to establish session keys, and some notable TLS implementations do so exclusively: e.g., <a href="/wiki/Gmail" title="Gmail">Gmail</a> and other Google HTTPS services that use <a href="/wiki/OpenSSL" title="OpenSSL">OpenSSL</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>169<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, many clients and servers supporting TLS (including browsers and web servers) are not configured to implement such restrictions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>170<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In practice, unless a web service uses DiffieHellman key exchange to implement forward secrecy, all of the encrypted web traffic to and from that service can be decrypted by a third party if it obtains the server's master (private) key; e.g., by means of a court order.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Even where DiffieHellman key exchange is implemented, server-side session management mechanisms can impact forward secrecy. The use of <a href="#Resumed_TLS_handshake">TLS session tickets</a> (a TLS extension) causes the session to be protected by AES128-CBC-SHA256 regardless of any other negotiated TLS parameters, including forward secrecy ciphersuites, and the long-lived TLS session ticket keys defeat the attempt to implement forward secrecy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-botchingpfs&#95;187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-botchingpfs-187"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ticketsecwp&#95;188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ticketsecwp-188"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ticketsecslides&#95;189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ticketsecslides-189"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>175<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Stanford University research in 2014 also found that of 473,802 TLS servers surveyed, 82.9% of the servers deploying ephemeral DiffieHellman (DHE) key exchange to support forward secrecy were using weak DiffieHellman parameters. These weak parameter choices could potentially compromise the effectiveness of the forward secrecy that the servers sought to provide.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>176<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Since late 2011, Google has provided forward secrecy with TLS by default to users of its <a href="/wiki/Gmail" title="Gmail">Gmail</a> service, along with <a href="/wiki/Google_Docs" title="Google Docs">Google Docs</a> and encrypted search, among other services.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-googleLongTerm&#95;191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-googleLongTerm-191"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Since November 2013, <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> has provided forward secrecy with TLS to users of its service.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>178<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of August&#160;2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, about 80% of TLS-enabled websites are configured to use cipher suites that provide forward secrecy to most web browsers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-trustworthy&#95;ssl&#95;pulse&#95;115-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trustworthy_ssl_pulse-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="TLS_interception">TLS interception</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: TLS interception"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Server_Name_Indication#Encrypted_Client_Hello" title="Server Name Indication">Server Name Indication &#160;Encrypted Client Hello</a></div>
<p>TLS interception (or <a href="/wiki/HTTPS" title="HTTPS">HTTPS</a> interception if applied particularly to that protocol) is the practice of intercepting an encrypted data stream in order to decrypt it, read and possibly manipulate it, and then re-encrypt it and send the data on its way again. This is done by way of a "<a href="/wiki/Transparent_proxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Transparent proxy">transparent proxy</a>": the interception software terminates the incoming TLS connection, inspects the HTTP plaintext, and then creates a new TLS connection to the destination.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Durumeric&#95;et&#95;al&#95;2017&#95;193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Durumeric_et_al_2017-193"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>TLS/HTTPS interception is used as an <a href="/wiki/Information_security" title="Information security">information security</a> measure by network operators in order to be able to scan for and protect against the intrusion of malicious content into the network, such as <a href="/wiki/Computer_virus" title="Computer virus">computer viruses</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Malware" title="Malware">malware</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Durumeric&#95;et&#95;al&#95;2017&#95;193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Durumeric_et_al_2017-193"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Such content could otherwise not be detected as long as it is protected by encryption, which is increasingly the case as a result of the routine use of HTTPS and other secure protocols.
</p><p>A significant drawback of TLS/HTTPS interception is that it introduces new security risks of its own. One notable limitation is that it provides a point where network traffic is available unencrypted thus giving attackers an incentive to attack this point in particular in order to gain access to otherwise secure content. The interception also allows the network operator, or persons who gain access to its interception system, to perform <a href="/wiki/Man-in-the-middle_attack" title="Man-in-the-middle attack">man-in-the-middle attacks</a> against network users. A 2017 study found that "HTTPS interception has become startlingly widespread, and that interception products as a class have a dramatically negative impact on connection security".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Durumeric&#95;et&#95;al&#95;2017&#95;193-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Durumeric_et_al_2017-193"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Protocol_details">Protocol details</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Protocol details"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The TLS protocol exchanges <i>records</i>, which encapsulate the data to be exchanged in a specific format (see below). Each record can be compressed, padded, appended with a <a href="/wiki/Message_authentication_code" title="Message authentication code">message authentication code</a> (MAC), or encrypted, all depending on the state of the connection. Each record has a <i>content type</i> field that designates the type of data encapsulated, a length field and a TLS version field. The data encapsulated may be control or procedural messages of the TLS itself, or simply the application data needed to be transferred by TLS. The specifications (cipher suite, keys etc.) required to exchange application data by TLS, are agreed upon in the "TLS handshake" between the client requesting the data and the server responding to requests. The protocol therefore defines both the structure of payloads transferred in TLS and the procedure to establish and monitor the transfer.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="TLS_handshake">TLS handshake</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: TLS handshake"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Full_TLS_1.2_Handshake.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Full_TLS_1.2_Handshake.svg/250px-Full_TLS_1.2_Handshake.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="234" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Full_TLS_1.2_Handshake.svg/500px-Full_TLS_1.2_Handshake.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="338" data-file-height="316" /></a><figcaption>Simplified illustration of the full TLS 1.2 handshake with timing information</figcaption></figure>
<p>When the connection starts, the record encapsulates a "control" protocol  the handshake messaging protocol (<i>content type</i> 22). This protocol is used to exchange all the information required by both sides for the exchange of the actual application data by TLS. It defines the format of messages and the order of their exchange. These may vary according to the demands of the client and server  i.e., there are several possible procedures to set up the connection. This initial exchange results in a successful TLS connection (both parties ready to transfer application data with TLS) or an alert message (as specified below).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Basic_TLS_handshake">Basic TLS handshake</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Basic TLS handshake"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A typical connection example follows, illustrating a <a href="/wiki/Handshake_(computing)" title="Handshake (computing)">handshake</a> where the server (but not the client) is authenticated by its certificate:
</p>
<ol><li>Negotiation phase:
<ul><li>A client sends a <b>ClientHello</b> message specifying the highest TLS protocol version it supports, a random number, a list of suggested <a href="/wiki/Cipher_suite" title="Cipher suite">cipher suites</a> and suggested compression methods. If the client is attempting to perform a resumed handshake, it may send a <i>session ID</i>. If the client can use <a href="/wiki/Application-Layer_Protocol_Negotiation" title="Application-Layer Protocol Negotiation">Application-Layer Protocol Negotiation</a>, it may include a list of supported application <a href="/wiki/Communications_protocol" class="mw-redirect" title="Communications protocol">protocols</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/HTTP/2" title="HTTP/2">HTTP/2</a>.</li>
<li>The server responds with a <b>ServerHello</b> message, containing the chosen protocol version, a random number, cipher suite and compression method from the choices offered by the client. To confirm or allow resumed handshakes the server may send a <i>session ID</i>. The chosen protocol version should be the highest that both the client and server support. For example, if the client supports TLS version 1.1 and the server supports version 1.2, version 1.1 should be selected; version 1.2 should not be selected.</li>
<li>The server sends its <b>Certificate</b> message (depending on the selected cipher suite, this may be omitted by the server).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-openpgp&#95;194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-openpgp-194"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>180<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>The server sends its <b>ServerKeyExchange</b> message (depending on the selected cipher suite, this may be omitted by the server). This message is sent for all <a href="/wiki/Diffie%E2%80%93Hellman_key_exchange" title="DiffieHellman key exchange">DHE</a>, <a href="/wiki/ECDHE" class="mw-redirect" title="ECDHE">ECDHE</a> and DH_anon cipher suites.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5246&#95;25-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5246-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>The server sends a <b>ServerHelloDone</b> message, indicating it is done with handshake negotiation.</li>
<li>The client responds with a <b>ClientKeyExchange</b> message, which may contain a <i>PreMasterSecret</i>, public key, or nothing. (Again, this depends on the selected cipher.) This <i>PreMasterSecret</i> is encrypted using the public key of the server certificate.</li>
<li>The client and server then use the random numbers and <i>PreMasterSecret</i> to compute a common secret, called the "master secret". All other key data (<a href="/wiki/Session_key" title="Session key">session keys</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Initialization_vector" title="Initialization vector">IV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Symmetric_encryption" class="mw-redirect" title="Symmetric encryption">symmetric encryption</a> key, <a href="/wiki/Message_authentication_code" title="Message authentication code">MAC</a> key<sup id="cite&#95;ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>181<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>) for this connection is derived from this master secret (and the client- and server-generated random values), which is passed through a carefully designed <a href="/wiki/Pseudorandomness" title="Pseudorandomness">pseudorandom</a> function.</li></ul></li>
<li>The client now sends a <b>ChangeCipherSpec</b> record, essentially telling the server, "Everything I tell you from now on will be authenticated (and encrypted if encryption parameters were present in the server certificate)." The ChangeCipherSpec is itself a record-level protocol with content type of 20.
<ul><li>The client sends an authenticated and encrypted <b>Finished</b> message, containing a hash and MAC over the previous handshake messages.</li>
<li>The server will attempt to decrypt the client's <i>Finished</i> message and verify the hash and MAC. If the decryption or verification fails, the handshake is considered to have failed and the connection should be terminated.</li></ul></li>
<li>Finally, the server sends a <b>ChangeCipherSpec</b>, telling the client, "Everything I tell you from now on will be authenticated (and encrypted, if encryption was negotiated)."
<ul><li>The server sends its authenticated and encrypted <b>Finished</b> message.</li>
<li>The client performs the same decryption and verification procedure as the server did in the previous step.</li></ul></li>
<li>Application phase: at this point, the "handshake" is complete and the application protocol is enabled, with content type of 23. Application messages exchanged between client and server will also be authenticated and optionally encrypted exactly like in their <i>Finished</i> message. Otherwise, the content type will return 25 and the client will not authenticate.</li></ol>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Client-authenticated_TLS_handshake">Client-authenticated TLS handshake</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Client-authenticated TLS handshake"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The following <i>full</i> example shows a client being authenticated (in addition to the server as in the example above; see <a href="/wiki/Mutual_authentication" title="Mutual authentication">mutual authentication</a>) via TLS using certificates exchanged between both peers.
</p>
<ol><li>Negotiation Phase:
<ul><li>A client sends a <b>ClientHello</b> message specifying the highest TLS protocol version it supports, a random number, a list of suggested cipher suites and compression methods.</li>
<li>The server responds with a <b>ServerHello</b> message, containing the chosen protocol version, a random number, cipher suite and compression method from the choices offered by the client. The server may also send a <i>session id</i> as part of the message to perform a resumed handshake.</li>
<li>The server sends its <b>Certificate</b> message (depending on the selected cipher suite, this may be omitted by the server).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-openpgp&#95;194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-openpgp-194"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>180<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>The server sends its <b>ServerKeyExchange</b> message (depending on the selected cipher suite, this may be omitted by the server). This message is sent for all DHE, ECDHE and DH_anon ciphersuites.<sup class="plainlinks nourlexpansion citation" id="ref&#95;5246"><a class="external autonumber" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security#endnote_5246">[1]</a></sup></li>
<li>The server sends a <b>CertificateRequest</b> message, to request a certificate from the client.</li>
<li>The server sends a <b>ServerHelloDone</b> message, indicating it is done with handshake negotiation.</li>
<li>The client responds with a <b>Certificate</b> message, which contains the client's certificate, but not its private key.</li>
<li>The client sends a <b>ClientKeyExchange</b> message, which may contain a <i>PreMasterSecret</i>, public key, or nothing. (Again, this depends on the selected cipher.) This <i>PreMasterSecret</i> is encrypted using the public key of the server certificate.</li>
<li>The client sends a <b>CertificateVerify</b> message, which is a signature over the previous handshake messages using the client's certificate's private key. This signature can be verified by using the client's certificate's public key. This lets the server know that the client has access to the private key of the certificate and thus owns the certificate.</li>
<li>The client and server then use the random numbers and <i>PreMasterSecret</i> to compute a common secret, called the "master secret". All other key data ("session keys") for this connection is derived from this master secret (and the client- and server-generated random values), which is passed through a carefully designed pseudorandom function.</li></ul></li>
<li>The client now sends a <b>ChangeCipherSpec</b> record, essentially telling the server, "Everything I tell you from now on will be authenticated (and encrypted if encryption was negotiated). "The ChangeCipherSpec is itself a record-level protocol and has type 20 and not 22.
<ul><li>Finally, the client sends an encrypted <b>Finished</b> message, containing a hash and MAC over the previous handshake messages.</li>
<li>The server will attempt to decrypt the client's <i>Finished</i> message and verify the hash and MAC. If the decryption or verification fails, the handshake is considered to have failed and the connection should be torn down.</li></ul></li>
<li>Finally, the server sends a <b>ChangeCipherSpec</b>, telling the client, "Everything I tell you from now on will be authenticated (and encrypted if encryption was negotiated)."
<ul><li>The server sends its own encrypted <b>Finished</b> message.</li>
<li>The client performs the same decryption and verification procedure as the server did in the previous step.</li></ul></li>
<li>Application phase: at this point, the "handshake" is complete and the application protocol is enabled, with content type of 23. Application messages exchanged between client and server will also be encrypted exactly like in their <i>Finished</i> message.</li></ol>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Resumed_TLS_handshake">Resumed TLS handshake</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Resumed TLS handshake"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Public key operations (e.g., RSA) are relatively expensive in terms of computational power. TLS provides a secure shortcut in the handshake mechanism to avoid these operations: resumed sessions. Resumed sessions are implemented using session IDs or session tickets.
</p><p>Apart from the performance benefit, resumed sessions can also be used for <a href="/wiki/Single_sign-on" title="Single sign-on">single sign-on</a>, as it guarantees that both the original session and any resumed session originate from the same client. This is of particular importance for the <a href="/wiki/FTPS" title="FTPS">FTP over TLS/SSL</a> protocol, which would otherwise suffer from a man-in-the-middle attack in which an attacker could intercept the contents of the secondary data connections.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>182<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="TLS_1.3_handshake">TLS 1.3 handshake</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: TLS 1.3 handshake"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The TLS 1.3 handshake was condensed to only one round trip compared to the two round trips required in previous versions of TLS/SSL.
</p><p>To start the handshake, the client guesses which key exchange algorithm will be selected by the server and sends a <b>ClientHello</b> message to the server containing a list of supported ciphers (in order of the client's preference) and public keys for some or all of its key exchange guesses. If the client successfully guesses the key exchange algorithm, 1 round trip is eliminated from the handshake. After receiving the <b>ClientHello</b>, the server selects a cipher and sends back a <b>ServerHello</b> with its own public key, followed by server <b>Certificate</b> and <b>Finished</b> messages.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>183<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>After the client receives the server's finished message, it now is coordinated with the server on which cipher suite to use.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>184<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading5"><h5 id="Session_IDs">Session IDs</h5><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Session IDs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In an ordinary <i>full</i> handshake, the server sends a <i>session id</i> as part of the <b>ServerHello</b> message. The client associates this <i>session id</i> with the server's IP address and TCP port, so that when the client connects again to that server, it can use the <i>session id</i> to shortcut the handshake. In the server, the <i>session id</i> maps to the cryptographic parameters previously negotiated, specifically the "master secret". Both sides must have the same "master secret" or the resumed handshake will fail (this prevents an eavesdropper from using a <i>session id</i>). The random data in the <b>ClientHello</b> and <b>ServerHello</b> messages virtually guarantee that the generated connection keys will be different from in the previous connection. In the RFCs, this type of handshake is called an <i>abbreviated</i> handshake. It is also described in the literature as a <i>restart</i> handshake.
</p>
<ol><li>Negotiation phase:
<ul><li>A client sends a <b>ClientHello</b> message specifying the highest TLS protocol version it supports, a random number, a list of suggested cipher suites and compression methods. Included in the message is the <i>session id</i> from the previous TLS connection.</li>
<li>The server responds with a <b>ServerHello</b> message, containing the chosen protocol version, a random number, cipher suite and compression method from the choices offered by the client. If the server recognizes the <i>session id</i> sent by the client, it responds with the same <i>session id</i>. The client uses this to recognize that a resumed handshake is being performed. If the server does not recognize the <i>session id</i> sent by the client, it sends a different value for its <i>session id</i>. This tells the client that a resumed handshake will not be performed. At this point, both the client and server have the "master secret" and random data to generate the key data to be used for this connection.</li></ul></li>
<li>The server now sends a <b>ChangeCipherSpec</b> record, essentially telling the client, "Everything I tell you from now on will be encrypted." The ChangeCipherSpec is itself a record-level protocol and has type 20 and not 22.
<ul><li>Finally, the server sends an encrypted <b>Finished</b> message, containing a hash and MAC over the previous handshake messages.</li>
<li>The client will attempt to decrypt the server's <i>Finished</i> message and verify the hash and MAC. If the decryption or verification fails, the handshake is considered to have failed and the connection should be torn down.</li></ul></li>
<li>Finally, the client sends a <b>ChangeCipherSpec</b>, telling the server, "Everything I tell you from now on will be encrypted."
<ul><li>The client sends its own encrypted <b>Finished</b> message.</li>
<li>The server performs the same decryption and verification procedure as the client did in the previous step.</li></ul></li>
<li>Application phase: at this point, the "handshake" is complete and the application protocol is enabled, with content type of 23. Application messages exchanged between client and server will also be encrypted exactly like in their <i>Finished</i> message.</li></ol>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading5"><h5 id="Session_tickets">Session tickets</h5><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Session tickets"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Instead of session IDs, TLS can also be extended via use of session tickets.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5077&#95;199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5077-199"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>185<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It defines a way to resume a TLS session without requiring that session-specific state is stored at the TLS server.
</p><p>When using session tickets, the TLS server stores its session-specific state in a session ticket and sends the session ticket to the TLS client for storing. The client resumes a TLS session by sending the session ticket to the server, and the server resumes the TLS session according to the session-specific state in the ticket. The session ticket is encrypted and authenticated by the server, and the server verifies its validity before using its contents.
</p><p>One particular weakness of this method with <a href="/wiki/OpenSSL" title="OpenSSL">OpenSSL</a> is that it always limits encryption and authentication security of the transmitted TLS session ticket to <code>AES128-CBC-SHA256</code>, no matter what other TLS parameters were negotiated for the actual TLS session.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ticketsecwp&#95;188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ticketsecwp-188"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This means that the state information (the TLS session ticket) is not as well protected as the TLS session itself. Of particular concern is OpenSSL's storage of the keys in an application-wide context (<code>SSL_CTX</code>), i.e. for the life of the application, and not allowing for re-keying of the <code>AES128-CBC-SHA256</code> TLS session tickets without resetting the application-wide OpenSSL context (which is uncommon, error-prone and often requires manual administrative intervention).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ticketsecslides&#95;189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ticketsecslides-189"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>175<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-botchingpfs&#95;187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-botchingpfs-187"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="TLS_record">TLS record</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: TLS record"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>This is the general format of all TLS records.
</p>

<dl><dt>Legacy version&#58; 16 bits</dt>
<dd>This field identifies the major and minor version of TLS prior to TLS 1.3 for the contained message. For a <b>ClientHello</b> message, this need not be the <i>highest</i> version supported by the client. For TLS 1.3 and later, this must be set to <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886049734">.mw-parser-output .monospaced{font-family:monospace,monospace}</style><span class="monospaced">0x0303</span> and application must send supported versions in an extra message extension block.</dd></dl>

<dl><dt>Length&#58; 16 bits&#59; Length &lt; 2<sup>14</sup></dt>
<dd>The length of <i>Protocol message(s)</i>, <i>MAC</i> and <i>Padding</i> fields combined. The length should not exceed 2<sup>14</sup> bytes (16 KiB).</dd>
<dt>Protocol message(s)&#58; variable</dt>
<dd>One or more messages identified by the Protocol field. Note that this field may be encrypted depending on the state of the connection. The length (in bytes) of all messages is indicated by the letter <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">m</span>.</dd>
<dt>Message Authentication Code<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>(MAC)&#58; 16, 20, or 32 bytes (optional)</dt>
<dd>A <a href="/wiki/Message_authentication_code" title="Message authentication code">message authentication code</a> computed over the <i>Protocol message(s)</i> field, with additional key material included. 32 bytes for the <a href="/wiki/SHA-256" class="mw-redirect" title="SHA-256">SHA-256</a>-based HMAC, 20 bytes for the <a href="/wiki/SHA-1" title="SHA-1">SHA-1</a>-based HMAC, 16 bytes for the <a href="/wiki/MD5" title="MD5">MD5</a>-based HMAC. Note that this field may be encrypted, or not included entirely, depending on the state of the connection. The length (in bytes) of the MAC is indicated by the letter <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">q</span>.</dd>
<dt>Padding&#58; variable (optional)</dt>
<dd>Padding is only added when needed.</dd></dl>
<p>No <i>MAC</i> or <i>Padding</i> fields can be present at end of TLS records before all cipher algorithms and parameters have been negotiated and handshaked and then confirmed by sending a <b>CipherStateChange</b> record (see below) for signalling that these parameters will take effect in all further records sent by the same peer.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Handshake_protocol">Handshake protocol</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Handshake protocol"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Most messages exchanged during the setup of the TLS session are based on this record, unless an error or warning occurs and needs to be signaled by an Alert protocol record (see below), or the encryption mode of the session is modified by another record (see <b>ChangeCipherSpec</b> protocol below).
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Alert_protocol">Alert protocol</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Alert protocol"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>This record should normally not be sent during normal handshaking or application exchanges. However, this message can be sent at any time during the handshake and up to the closure of the session. If this is used to signal a fatal error, the session will be closed immediately after sending this record, so this record is used to give a reason for this closure. If the alert level is flagged as a warning, the remote can decide to close the session if it decides that the session is not reliable enough for its needs (before doing so, the remote may also send its own signal).
</p>

<tr>
<th style="width:35px;">&#8942;

<th style="width:30px;">&#8942;
</th>
<td colspan="32" style="background: oldlace;"><i>Padding (block ciphers only)</i>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<dl><dt>Content Type&#58; 8 bits&#59; == 21</dt>
<dd>This field indicates the <b>Alert</b> Protocol Type.</dd>
<dt>Length&#58; 16 bits&#59; == 2</dt>
<dd>The length of the rest of fields, which is 2.</dd>
<dt>Level&#58; 8 bits</dt>
<dd>This field identifies the level of alert. If the level is fatal, the sender should close the session immediately. Otherwise, the recipient may decide to terminate the session itself, by sending its own fatal alert and closing the session itself immediately after sending it. The use of Alert records is optional, however if it is missing before the session closure, the session may be resumed automatically (with its handshakes).</dd>
<dd>Normal closure of a session after termination of the transported application should preferably be alerted with at least the <i>Close notify</i> Alert type (with a simple warning level) to prevent such automatic resume of a new session. Signalling explicitly the normal closure of a secure session before effectively closing its transport layer is useful to prevent or detect attacks (like attempts to truncate the securely transported data, if it intrinsically does not have a predetermined length or duration that the recipient of the secured data may expect).</dd></dl>

<dd>This field identifies which type of alert is being sent.</dd></dl>

<th style="width:35px;"><span style="white-space: nowrap;"><i><span class="nowrap">&#8288;<span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle 5+m+q}">

</th>
<th style="width:30px;"><span style="white-space: nowrap;"><i><span class="nowrap">&#8288;<span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle 40+(m+q)*8}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mn>40</mn>
        <mo>+</mo>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>m</mi>
        <mo>+</mo>
        <mi>q</mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <mo>&#x2217;<!--  --></mo>
        <mn>8</mn>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 40+(m+q)*8}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d44818cad7a7b6896983456c68c4f124c1e41c7b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:16.282ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle 40+(m+q)*8}"></span>&#8288;</span></i></span>
</th>
<td colspan="32" style="background: oldlace;"><i>Padding (block ciphers only)</i>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<dl><dt>Content Type&#58; 8 bits&#59; == 23</dt>
<dd>This field identifies the <b>Application</b> Protocol Type.</dd>
<dt>Length&#58; 16 bits&#59; Length &lt; 2<sup>14</sup></dt>
<dd>The length of <i>Application Data</i>, <i>MAC</i> and <i>Padding</i> fields combined. The length should not exceed 2<sup>14</sup> bytes (16 KiB).</dd>
<dt>Application Data&#58; variable</dt>
<dd>Data of the application. The length (in bytes) of the data is indicated by the letter <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">m</span>.</dd>
<dt>Message Authentication Code<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>(MAC)&#58; 16, 20, or 32 bytes (optional)</dt>
<dd>A <a href="/wiki/Message_authentication_code" title="Message authentication code">message authentication code</a> computed over the <i>Application Data</i> field. 32 bytes for the <a href="/wiki/SHA-256" class="mw-redirect" title="SHA-256">SHA-256</a>-based HMAC, 20 bytes for the <a href="/wiki/SHA-1" title="SHA-1">SHA-1</a>-based HMAC, 16 bytes for the <a href="/wiki/MD5" title="MD5">MD5</a>-based HMAC. The length (in bytes) of the MAC is indicated by the letter <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">q</span>.</dd>
<dt>Padding&#58; variable (optional)</dt>
<dd>The last byte contains the padding length.</dd></dl>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Support_for_name-based_virtual_servers">Support for name-based virtual servers</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Support for name-based virtual servers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>From the application protocol point of view, TLS belongs to a lower layer, although the TCP/IP model is too coarse to show it. This means that the TLS handshake is usually (except in the <a href="/wiki/STARTTLS" class="mw-redirect" title="STARTTLS">STARTTLS</a> case) performed before the application protocol can start. In the <a href="/wiki/Virtual_domain" class="mw-redirect" title="Virtual domain">name-based virtual server</a> feature being provided by the application layer, all co-hosted virtual servers share the same certificate because the server has to select and send a certificate immediately after the ClientHello message. This is a big problem in hosting environments because it means either sharing the same certificate among all customers or using a different IP address for each of them.
</p><p>There are two known workarounds provided by <a href="/wiki/X.509" title="X.509">X.509</a>:
</p>
<ul><li>If all virtual servers belong to the same domain, a <a href="/wiki/Wildcard_certificate" class="mw-redirect" title="Wildcard certificate">wildcard certificate</a> can be used.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>186<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Besides the loose host name selection that might be a problem or not, there is no common agreement about how to match wildcard certificates. Different rules are applied depending on the application protocol or software used.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>187<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Add every virtual host name in the subjectAltName extension. The major problem being that the certificate needs to be reissued whenever a new virtual server is added.</li></ul>
<p>To provide the server name, Transport Layer Security (TLS) Extensions allow clients to include a <a href="/wiki/Server_Name_Indication" title="Server Name Indication">Server Name Indication</a> extension (SNI) in the extended ClientHello message.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc6066&#95;202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc6066-202"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>188<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: 3">&#58;&#8202;3&#8202;</span></sup> This extension hints to the server immediately which name the client wishes to connect to, so the server can select the appropriate certificate to send to the clients.
</p><p>There is also a method to implement name-based virtual hosting by upgrading HTTP to TLS via an <a href="/wiki/HTTP/1.1_Upgrade_header" title="HTTP/1.1 Upgrade header">HTTP/1.1 Upgrade header</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc2817&#95;2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc2817-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Normally this is to securely implement HTTP over TLS within the main "http" <a href="/wiki/URI_scheme" class="mw-redirect" title="URI scheme">URI scheme</a> instead of the commonly used "https" scheme. This would avoid forking the URI space and reduces the number of used ports, however, few implementations currently support this.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>

<li><a href="/wiki/Certificate_Transparency" title="Certificate Transparency">Certificate Transparency</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Private_Communications_Technology" title="Private Communications Technology">Private Communications Technology</a> (PCT)  a historic Microsoft competitor to SSL 2.0</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/QUIC" title="QUIC">QUIC</a> (Quick UDP Internet Connections)  "was designed to provide security protection equivalent to TLS/SSL"; QUIC's main goal is to improve perceived performance of connection-oriented web applications that are currently using TCP</li>

<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://datatracker.ietf.org/wg/tls">Internet Engineering Task Force  TLS Workgroup</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140111193101/http://datatracker.ietf.org/wg/tls/">Archived</a> 2014-01-11 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="IETF_Request_for_comments"><a href="/wiki/IETF" class="mw-redirect" title="IETF">IETF</a> <a href="/wiki/Request_for_comments" class="mw-redirect" title="Request for comments">Request for comments</a></h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: IETF Request for comments"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Primary_standards">Primary standards</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Primary standards"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The current approved version of (D)TLS is version 1.3, which is specified in:
</p>
<ul><li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8446">8446</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>The Transport Layer Security (TLS) Protocol Version 1.3,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc8446&#95;6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc8446-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9147">9147</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>The Datagram Transport Layer Security (DTLS) Protocol Version 1.3,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9147&#95;11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9147-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li></ul>
<p>The current standards replaces these former versions:
</p>
<ul><li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5246">5246</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>The Transport Layer Security (TLS) Protocol Version 1.2,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5246&#95;25-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5246-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Obsolete.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc6347">6347</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Datagram Transport Layer Security Version 1.2,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc6347&#95;8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc6347-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Obsolete.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc4346">4346</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>The Transport Layer Security (TLS) Protocol Version 1.1,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc4346&#95;46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc4346-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Historic.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc2246">2246</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>The TLS Protocol Version 1.0,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc2246&#95;203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc2246-203"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>189<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Historic.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc6101">6101</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>The Secure Sockets Layer (SSL) Protocol Version 3.0,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc6101&#95;204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc6101-204"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>190<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Historic.</i></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//tools.ietf.org/html/draft-hickman-netscape-ssl-00">Internet Draft (1995)</a>: "The SSL Protocol"</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Extensions">Extensions</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport_Layer_Security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Extensions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Other <a href="/wiki/Request_for_Comments" title="Request for Comments">RFCs</a> subsequently extended (D)TLS.
</p><p>Extensions to (D)TLS 1.3 include:
</p>
<ul><li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9367">9367</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>GOST Cipher Suites for Transport Layer Security (TLS) Protocol Version 1.3,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9367&#95;87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9367-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Informational.</i></li></ul>
<p>Extensions to (D)TLS 1.2 include:
</p>
<ul><li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5288">5288</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>AES <a href="/wiki/Galois/Counter_Mode" title="Galois/Counter Mode">Galois Counter Mode</a> (GCM) Cipher Suites for TLS,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5288&#95;92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5288-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5289">5289</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>TLS Elliptic Curve Cipher Suites with SHA-256/384 and AES Galois Counter Mode (GCM),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5289&#95;93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5289-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5746">5746</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Transport Layer Security (TLS) Renegotiation Indication Extension,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5746&#95;125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5746-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5878">5878</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Transport Layer Security (TLS) Authorization Extensions,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5878&#95;205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5878-205"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>191<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Experimental.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5932">5932</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Camellia Cipher Suites for TLS,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5932&#95;99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5932-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc6066">6066</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Transport Layer Security (TLS) Extensions: Extension Definitions,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc6066&#95;202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc6066-202"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>188<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996643573">.mw-parser-output .block-indent{padding-left:3em;padding-right:0;overflow:hidden}</style><div class="block-indent">Includes <a href="/wiki/Server_Name_Indication" title="Server Name Indication">Server Name Indication</a> and <a href="/wiki/OCSP_stapling" title="OCSP stapling">OCSP stapling</a>.</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc6091">6091</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Using <a href="/wiki/OpenPGP" class="mw-redirect" title="OpenPGP">OpenPGP</a> Keys for Transport Layer Security (TLS) Authentication,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc6091&#95;206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc6091-206"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>192<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Informational.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc6176">6176</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Prohibiting Secure Sockets Layer (SSL) Version 2.0,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc6176&#95;19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc6176-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc6209">6209</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Addition of the <a href="/wiki/ARIA_(cipher)" title="ARIA (cipher)">ARIA</a> Cipher Suites to Transport Layer Security (TLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc6209&#95;100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc6209-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Informational.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc6347">6347</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Datagram Transport Layer Security Version 1.2,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc6347&#95;8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc6347-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Obsolete.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">The definition is now part of the DTLS 1.3 specification.</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc6367">6367</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Addition of the <a href="/wiki/Camellia_(cipher)" title="Camellia (cipher)">Camellia</a> Cipher Suites to Transport Layer Security (TLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc6367&#95;98-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc6367-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Informational.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc6460">6460</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite><a href="/wiki/Suite_B" class="mw-redirect" title="Suite B">Suite B</a> Profile for Transport Layer Security (TLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc6460&#95;207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc6460-207"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>193<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Historic.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">Support for Suite B Cryptography has been discontinued by the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a>.</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc6655">6655</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>AES-CCM Cipher Suites for Transport Layer Security (TLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc6655&#95;95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc6655-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc7027">7027</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Elliptic Curve Cryptography (ECC) Brainpool Curves for Transport Layer Security (TLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc7027&#95;208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc7027-208"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>194<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Informational.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc7251">7251</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>AES-CCM <a href="/wiki/Elliptic-curve_cryptography" title="Elliptic-curve cryptography">Elliptic Curve Cryptography</a> (ECC) Cipher Suites for TLS,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc7251&#95;96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc7251-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Informational.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc7301">7301</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Transport Layer Security (TLS) <a href="/wiki/Application-Layer_Protocol_Negotiation" title="Application-Layer Protocol Negotiation">Application-Layer Protocol Negotiation</a> Extension,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc7301&#95;209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc7301-209"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>195<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc7366">7366</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Encrypt-then-MAC for Transport Layer Security (TLS) and Datagram Transport Layer Security (DTLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc7366&#95;153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc7366-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc7465">7465</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Prohibiting RC4 Cipher Suites,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc7465&#95;112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc7465-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc7507">7507</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>TLS Fallback Signaling Cipher Suite Value (SCSV) for Preventing Protocol Downgrade Attacks,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc7507&#95;210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc7507-210"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>196<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Obsolete.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc7568">7568</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Deprecating Secure Sockets Layer Version 3.0,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc7568&#95;20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc7568-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc7627">7627</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Transport Layer Security (TLS) Session Hash and Extended Master Secret Extension,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc7627&#95;211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc7627-211"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>197<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc7685">7685</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>A Transport Layer Security (TLS) ClientHello Padding Extension,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc7685&#95;212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc7685-212"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>198<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc8422">8422</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Elliptic Curve Cryptography (ECC) Cipher Suites for Transport Layer Security (TLS) Versions 1.2 and Earlier,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc8422&#95;84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc8422-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc9189">9189</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>GOST Cipher Suites for Transport Layer Security (TLS) Protocol Version 1.2,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc9189&#95;86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc9189-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Informational.</i></li></ul>
<p>Extensions to (D)TLS 1.1 include:
</p>
<ul><li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc4366">4366</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Transport Layer Security (TLS) Extensions,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc4366&#95;213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc4366-213"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>199<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Obsolete.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">Describes both a set of specific extensions and a generic extension mechanism.</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc4492">4492</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite><a href="/wiki/Elliptic_Curve_Cryptography" class="mw-redirect" title="Elliptic Curve Cryptography">Elliptic Curve Cryptography</a> (ECC) Cipher Suites for Transport Layer Security (TLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc4492&#95;214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc4492-214"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>200<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Obsolete.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc4680">4680</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>TLS Handshake Message for Supplemental Data,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc4680&#95;215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc4680-215"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>201<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc4681">4681</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>TLS User Mapping Extension,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc4681&#95;216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc4681-216"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>202<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc4785">4785</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Pre-Shared Key (PSK) Ciphersuites with NULL Encryption for Transport Layer Security (TLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc4785&#95;217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc4785-217"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>203<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5054">5054</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Using the <a href="/wiki/Secure_Remote_Password" class="mw-redirect" title="Secure Remote Password">Secure Remote Password</a> (SRP) Protocol for TLS Authentication,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5054&#95;218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5054-218"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>204<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Informational.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">Defines the <a href="/wiki/TLS-SRP" title="TLS-SRP">TLS-SRP</a> ciphersuites.</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5077">5077</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Transport Layer Security (TLS) Session Resumption without Server-Side State,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5077&#95;199-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5077-199"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>185<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Obsolete.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5081">5081</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Using OpenPGP Keys for Transport Layer Security (TLS) Authentication,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5081&#95;219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5081-219"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>205<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Experimental.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc5216">5216</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>The <a href="/wiki/Extensible_Authentication_Protocol" title="Extensible Authentication Protocol">EAP</a>-TLS Authentication Protocol,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc5216&#95;220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc5216-220"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>206<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li></ul>
<p>Extensions to TLS 1.0 include:
</p>
<ul><li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc2595">2595</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Using TLS with IMAP, POP3 and ACAP,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc2595&#95;221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc2595-221"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>207<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">Specifies an extension to the IMAP, POP3 and ACAP services that allow the server and client to use transport-layer security to provide private, authenticated communication over the Internet.</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc2712">2712</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Addition of <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(protocol)" title="Kerberos (protocol)">Kerberos</a> Cipher Suites to Transport Layer Security (TLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc2712&#95;222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc2712-222"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>208<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">The 40-bit cipher suites defined in this memo appear only for the purpose of documenting the fact that those cipher suite codes have already been assigned.</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc2817">2817</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Upgrading to TLS Within HTTP/1.1,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc2817&#95;2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc2817-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">Explains how to use the <a href="/wiki/HTTP/1.1_Upgrade_header" title="HTTP/1.1 Upgrade header">Upgrade mechanism in HTTP/1.1</a> to initiate Transport Layer Security (TLS) over an existing TCP connection. This allows unsecured and secured HTTP traffic to share the same <i>well known</i> port (in this case, http: at 80 rather than https: at 443).</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc2818">2818</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>HTTP Over TLS,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc2818&#95;223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc2818-223"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>209<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Obsolete.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">Distinguishes secured traffic from insecure traffic by the use of a different 'server port'.</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc3207">3207</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>SMTP Service Extension for Secure SMTP over Transport Layer Security,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc3207&#95;224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc3207-224"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>210<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">Specifies an extension to the SMTP service that allows an SMTP server and client to use transport-layer security to provide private, authenticated communication over the Internet.</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc3268">3268</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Advanced Encryption Standard (AES) Ciphersuites for Transport Layer Security (TLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc3268&#95;225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc3268-225"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>211<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Obsolete.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">Adds <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Encryption_Standard" title="Advanced Encryption Standard">Advanced Encryption Standard</a> (AES) cipher suites to the previously existing symmetric ciphers.</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc3546">3546</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Transport Layer Security (TLS) Extensions,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc3546&#95;226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc3546-226"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>212<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Obsolete.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">Adds a mechanism for negotiating protocol extensions during session initialisation and defines some extensions.</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc3749">3749</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Transport Layer Security Protocol Compression Methods,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc3749&#95;227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc3749-227"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>213<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">Specifies the framework for compression methods and the <a href="/wiki/DEFLATE" class="mw-redirect" title="DEFLATE">DEFLATE</a> compression method.</div></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc3943">3943</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Transport Layer Security (TLS) Protocol Compression Using Lempel-Ziv-Stac (LZS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc3943&#95;228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc3943-228"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>214<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Informational.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc4132">4132</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Addition of <a href="/wiki/Camellia_(cipher)" title="Camellia (cipher)">Camellia</a> Cipher Suites to Transport Layer Security (TLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc4132&#95;229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc4132-229"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>215<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Obsolete.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc4162">4162</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Addition of SEED Cipher Suites to Transport Layer Security (TLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc4162&#95;101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc4162-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc4217">4217</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Securing FTP with TLS,</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc4217&#95;230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc4217-230"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>216<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" />RFC&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rfc-editor.org/rfc/rfc4279">4279</a>&#8199;&#8211;&#8199;"<cite>Pre-Shared Key Ciphersuites for Transport Layer Security (TLS),</cite>"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rfc4279&#95;231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rfc4279-231"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>217<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Proposed Standard.</i> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996643573" /><div class="block-indent">Adds three sets of new cipher suites for the TLS protocol to support authentication based on pre-shared keys.</div></li></ul>
